Alice and Bazza are playing the inekoalaty game, a two-player game whose rules depend on a positive real number $\lambda$ which is known to both players. On the $n$th turn of the game (starting with $n=1$) the following happens:
\begin{itemize}
    \item If $n$ is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number $x_n$ such that
\[

\]
    \item If $n$ is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number $x_n$ such that
\[
x_1^2 + x_2^2 + \cdots + x_n^2 \le n.
\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

The pattern suggests that if both play optimally, the game can continue indefinitely for λ=0.8. But we need to determine the critical λ where the game is balanced. Perhaps the answer is: Alice wins if λ > 2? But we already saw λ=1 gives her win. So that's not right.

Maybe we misinterpreted the game. The rule: "If n is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1 + ... + x_n ≤ λ n." That means that on her turn, she must choose x_n such that the cumulative sum up to n is ≤ λ n. But note that after Bazza's turn, the sum is not constrained. So she can choose x_n to be 0, and then the sum after her turn might be less than λ n, but then she might be able to increase later. However, the condition is only that the sum after her choice is ≤ λ n. So she can always choose 0 if the previous sum is already ≤ λ n? But the previous sum is after Bazza's turn, which could be > λ n? Actually if after Bazza's turn, the sum is > λ n, then she cannot choose any nonnegative x_n because the sum would be at least that > λ n. So she loses. So she must ensure that after Bazza's turn, the sum is ≤ λ n. That's her constraint. So the game is about controlling the sum after each of Bazza's turns to keep it within λ times the odd index. Similarly, Bazza must ensure that after his turn, the sum of squares is ≤ n, and also he must ensure that after his turn, the sum (which is used by Alice) is not too high to cause her loss.

Thus the players have control over the increments. Let's denote the state before each player's turn.

Define k = 1,2,3,... Let's consider the state after each even turn (Bazza's turn). Let n=2k. Then we have S_{2k} = sum_{i=1}^{2k} x_i, and Q_{2k} = sum_{i=1}^{2k} x_i^2. The constraint for Bazza at his turn (n=2k) is Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. But note that Q_{2k} is after his choice, so he must satisfy Q_{2k-1} + x_{2k}^2 ≤ 2k. He can choose x_{2k} to be any nonnegative number such that the inequality holds. So his move determines S_{2k} and Q_{2k}. Then Alice's turn at n=2k+1: she must choose x_{2k+1} such that S_{2k+1} ≤ λ (2k+1). She can choose any nonnegative x_{2k+1} such that S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} + x_{2k+1} ≤ λ (2k+1). So she needs S_{2k} ≤ λ (2k+1) to have any move. If S_{2k} > λ (2k+1), she loses. So Bazza wants to make S_{2k} as large as possible, but limited by the quadratic constraint. He can choose x_{2k} to increase S_{2k}. Similarly, Alice wants to make Q_{2k+1} as large as possible, so that on Bazza's next turn (n=2k+2), she can cause Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, making him lose. But she also must keep S_{2k+1} ≤ λ (2k+1). So she can try to increase Q_{2k+1} by choosing a large x_{2k+1}. However, she is limited by the sum constraint: she can choose x_{2k+1} up to λ (2k+1) - S_{2k}. So she can increase Q by that amount, but the quadratic contribution is x^2. So she can choose x_{2k+1} = λ (2k+1) - S_{2k} (the maximum allowed) to make S_{2k+1} = λ (2k+1) exactly. This is often optimal for her because it gives her the largest possible Q increase. So she will likely set S_{2k+1} = λ (2k+1) (if possible). Then Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + (λ (2k+1) - S_{2k})^2. So the state after her turn is determined by the previous state.

Thus the game can be analyzed by the recurrence:

Let s_k = S_{2k} (after Bazza's turn), q_k = Q_{2k} (after Bazza's turn). Bazza chooses x_{2k} (call it b_k) such that q_k + b_k^2 ≤ 2k. Then he sets s_k = S_{2k-1} + b_k, and q_k = Q_{2k-1} + b_k^2? Wait careful: The state after Bazza's turn is (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}). But the state before his turn is (S_{2k-1}, Q_{2k-1}). He chooses b_k = x_{2k} such that Q_{2k-1} + b_k^2 ≤ 2k. Then S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + b_k, Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + b_k^2 (he can choose to make Q_{2k} exactly 2k if he wants, but he might also choose less). So after his turn, we have s_k = S_{2k}, q_k = Q_{2k}. Then Alice's turn: she chooses a_k = x_{2k+1} such that S_{2k+1} = s_k + a_k ≤ λ (2k+1). She will likely set a_k = λ (2k+1) - s_k (maximum allowed) to maximize Q increase. Then Q_{2k+1} = q_k + a_k^2. Then Bazza's next turn: he sees state (S_{2k+1}, Q_{2k+1}) and chooses b_{k+1}. So we have a recurrence.

Thus we can consider the game as a two-player game where each player chooses a number that determines the next state. The players want to force a win: Alice wants to eventually make Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2 (so that Bazza cannot move at n=2k+2). Bazza wants to eventually make S_{2k} > λ (2k+1) (so that Alice cannot move at n=2k+1). But note that if Bazza makes S_{2k} > λ (2k+1), then Alice loses immediately at her next turn. So Bazza's winning condition is that at some k, S_{2k} > λ (2k+1). Similarly, Alice's winning condition is that at some k, Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2.

Now we need to find for which λ Alice has a strategy to force Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2 for some k, regardless of Bazza's play. And for which λ Bazza has a strategy to force S_{2k} > λ (2k+1) for some k, regardless of Alice's play.

If both players play optimally, the game may be a draw if neither can force a win indefinitely.

We can try to derive a necessary condition for Bazza to avoid losing indefinitely. If Bazza can always make a move, then we must have for all k, S_{2k} ≤ λ (2k+1) (so Alice can move). Also, Q_{2k} ≤ 2k (by his move). And he can choose his moves to maintain these. Alice can choose her moves to try to increase Q_{2k+1} beyond 2k+2.

Suppose Bazza plays optimally to avoid losing. He might choose x_{2k} to minimize the chance that Alice can overflow at the next odd turn. That is, he wants to make S_{2k} as large as possible? Actually if he makes S_{2k} large, that could cause Alice to lose at her next turn if S_{2k} > λ (2k+1). But he also wants to avoid that Alice can later make Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2. So he might want to keep Q_{2k} small, but he can also choose x_{2k} to influence both. Let's analyze the recurrence.

We can define the "score" for Alice after her turn: Q_{2k+1} - (2k+2). She wants this to be positive. Given Q_{2k} and S_{2k}, she can choose a = x_{2k+1} ∈ [0, λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}]. The maximum Q she can achieve is Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2. So she will try to make Q_{2k+1} as large as possible. So we can define the "overflow" value O_k = Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2 - (2k+2). She wants O_k > 0 for some k. Bazza wants to avoid that for all k.

Bazza's move: given Q_{2k-1} and S_{2k-1}, he chooses b = x_{2k} ∈ [0, sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1})]. He will choose b to minimize the maximum possible overflow that Alice can cause at the next turn. That is, he wants to minimize the maximum over his choice of the quantity Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2 - (2k+2). But note that S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + b, and Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + b^2. So we have a function f(b) = (Q_{2k-1} + b^2) + (λ(2k+1) - (S_{2k-1} + b))^2 - (2k+2). He wants to choose b to minimize the maximum? Actually he wants to avoid that Alice can make overflow > 0. So he wants to make sure that for the b he chooses, the maximum possible overflow (by Alice) is ≤ 0. Since Alice will then choose a to maximize Q, he must ensure that even if Alice chooses the maximum a, the overflow is ≤ 0. So his condition is: he must choose b such that Q_{2k-1} + b^2 + (λ(2k+1) - (S_{2k-1} + b))^2 ≤ 2k+2. If he can choose b satisfying that, then Alice cannot overflow at that turn. He also must respect that b^2 ≤ 2k - Q_{2k-1}. So he wants to see if there exists b ∈ [0, sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1})] such that the inequality holds. If there is no such b, then Alice can force a win at that turn. So the game reduces to: given the state (S_{2k-1}, Q_{2k-1}), can Bazza choose b to satisfy the inequality? If not, Alice wins. Conversely, if Bazza can choose b such that the inequality holds, then the game continues. Alice's turn then determines the next state (S_{2k+1}, Q_{2k+1}) = (λ(2k+1), Q_{2k-1}+b^2+(λ(2k+1)-S_{2k-1}-b)^2). But note that after Alice's turn, we have S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1), Q_{2k+1} = q_{2k-1} + b^2 + a^2, where a = λ(2k+1)-S_{2k-1}-b. So the state after her turn is determined.

Thus we can view the game as a deterministic process if both players play optimally: Bazza chooses b to minimize the chance of overflow, and Alice then sets a to maximize Q. So the critical λ is the one where the inequality is always satisfiable (i.e., Bazza can avoid losing) for all k, but if λ is larger, then at some point Bazza cannot avoid.

We can try to find a condition on λ such that there exists a strategy for Bazza to keep the game going forever. Perhaps it's easier to think in terms of "potential" and use the idea of "alternating inequalities" to determine the outcome. This is reminiscent of a known problem: "Alice and Bob play a game where Alice chooses x_n with sum ≤ λn, Bob chooses x_n with sum of squares ≤ n. Determine λ." Let's re-read the problem statement: "On the nth turn of the game (starting with n=1) the following happens:
- If n is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1 + x_2 + ... + x_n ≤ λ n.
- If n is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1^2 + x_2^2 + ... + x_n^2 ≤ n."
If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

So the game is an infinite game; we want to find λ where Alice can force a win (i.e., she can ensure that Bazza eventually cannot move) or Bazza can force a win.

From our tests, it seems that for λ=0.1, Bazza wins. For λ=0.8, we saw that if both play optimally (Bazza playing λ each turn, Alice playing max a each turn), the game can go on forever. So his strategy is to choose b_k to minimize the quantity Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2, subject to b_k^2 ≤ 2k - Q_{2k-1}. But note that S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + b_k, and Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + b_k^2. So the expression he wants to minimize is Q_{2k-1} + b_k^2 + (λ(2k+1) - (S_{2k-1}+b_k))^2 = Q_{2k-1} + b_k^2 + (2λ - b_k)^2, since S_{2k-1}=λ(2k-1). So his objective is to minimize f(b)=b^2+(2λ-b)^2 = 2b^2-4λb+4λ^2, over b ∈ [0, sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1})]. The minimum is at b=λ, giving f(λ)=2λ^2. But he can only choose b=λ if λ ≤ sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}). If that condition holds, he will choose b=λ, and then the overflow value O_k = Q_{2k-1} + 2λ^2 - (2k+2) = (Q_{2k-1} + 2λ^2) - (2k+2). If he cannot choose b=λ, then he will choose b = sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}) (the maximum allowed), and then the overflow becomes Q_{2k-1} + (2k - Q_{2k-1}) + (2λ - sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}))^2 = 2k + (2λ - sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}))^2. Then he will compare this to 2k+2.

So Bazza cannot avoid that Alice can overflow at n=7. So for λ=0.9, Alice has a winning strategy: she can play x_1=0.9, then x_3=0, and then whatever Bazza does, she can win at n=7. But we need to check if Bazza can choose a different b_1 at n=2 to avoid this. So far we assumed he plays b_1=0.9. He could choose a different b_1. After Alice's x_1=0.9, he chooses b_1 ∈ [0, sqrt(2-0.81)=1.091]. He wants to make sure that after his move, he can later avoid overflow. He can choose b_1 to influence the subsequent states. Let's analyze from the start.

We have λ=0.9. Alice chooses x_1=0.9 (max). Then S_1=0.9, Q_1=0.81. Bazza chooses b_1 = x_2. Then after his move, S_2 = 0.9+b_1, Q_2 = 0.81+b_1^2. Then Alice at n=3 chooses x_3. She wants to eventually cause overflow. She can choose x_3=0 as before. Then S_3 = 0.9+b_1, Q_3 = 0.81+b_1^2. Then Bazza at n=4: he chooses b_2 ∈ [0, sqrt(4 - Q_3)]. He wants to avoid overflow later. Then we need to see if Alice can win at n=5 or n=7. The recurrence: Q_3 = 0.81+b_1^2. Then after Bazza's move at n=4, we have S_4 = S_3 + b_2 = 0.9+b_1+b_2, Q_4 = Q_3 + b_2^2 = 0.81+b_1^2+b_2^2. Then Alice at n=5: she can choose a_2 = 4.5 - S_4 = 4.5 - (0.9+b_1+b_2) = 3.6 - b_1 - b_2. Then Q_5 = Q_4 + a_2^2 = 0.81+b_1^2+b_2^2 + (3.6 - b_1 - b_2)^2. She wants Q_5 > 6 to win at n=5. But note that Bazza can choose b_2 to try to keep Q_5 ≤ 6. We need to see if Bazza can always choose b_2 such that Q_5 ≤ 6. If he can, then Alice cannot win at n=5. Then she might try at n=7. So we need to analyze the game more generally.

This is getting quite involved. Perhaps there is a known result that the critical λ is 2, and for λ > 2, Alice wins; for λ < 2, Bazza wins; at λ=2, it's a draw. But our tests show that for λ=1, Alice wins, which contradicts that. Unless we miscomputed λ=1? Let's double-check λ=1. We thought that Alice can win by setting x_3=0. But is that correct? Let's simulate carefully for λ=1. Alice at n=1: she chooses x_1. The constraint: x_1 ≤ 1. She can choose any nonnegative number ≤1. She wants to win. She might choose x_1=1. Then S_1=1, Q_1=1. Bazza at n=2: must choose x_2 such that 1+x_2^2 ≤2, so x_2 ≤1. He can choose any x_2 ∈ [0,1]. Suppose he chooses x_2=1. Then S_2=2, Q_2=2. Alice at n=3: she must choose x_3 such that S_3 ≤3. So she can choose x_3=0. Then S_3=2, Q_3=2. Bazza at n=4: he must choose x_4 such that Q_4 ≤4. Q_3=2, so x_4^2 ≤2, so x_4 ≤√2≈1.414. He can choose any x_4 ∈ [0,1.414]. Suppose he chooses x_4=1.414. Then S_4=2+1.414=3.414, Q_4=2+2=4. Alice at n=5: she must choose x_5 such that S_5 ≤5. So she can choose x_5 = 5 - 3.414 = 1.586. Then Q_5 = Q_4 + (1.586)^2 = 4 + 2.516 = 6.516. That's >6, so Bazza at n=6 must have Q_6 ≤6, but Q_5=6.516 >6, so he cannot move. Thus Alice wins. Could Bazza choose a different x_4 to avoid this? For example, he could choose x_4=0. Then S_4=2, Q_4=2. Then Alice at n=5: she can choose x_5 = 5 - 2 = 3, then Q_5=2+9=11>6, win. If he chooses x_4=1, then S_4=3, Q_4=2+1=3. Then Alice at n=5: x_5 = 5-3=2, Q_5=3+4=7>6, win. If he chooses x_4=1.2, then S_4=3.2, Q_4=2+1.44=3.44, then x_5=5-3.2=1.8, Q_5=3.44+3.24=6.68>6, win. So no matter what, Alice can win by setting x_5 = 5 - S_4. So for λ=1, Alice wins. So the threshold is less than 1. What about λ=0.9? We need to see if Alice can also win. Let's try a strategy: She chooses x_1=0.9 (max). Then Bazza at n=2 can choose any b_1 ≤ sqrt(2-0.81)=1.091. He might choose b_1=0.9 to keep the game safe. Then she sets x_3=0. Then Bazza at n=4: he can choose b_2 ≤ sqrt(4 - 1.62)=sqrt2.38=1.543. He might choose b_2=1.35 (as we considered). Then she sets x_5 = 4.5 - S_4 = 4.5 - (0.9+0.9+1.35)=4.5 - 3.15=1.35. Then Q_5 = Q_4 + 1.35^2 = (0.81+0.81+1.8225) + 1.8225? Wait compute: Q_4 = 0.81 + b_1^2 + b_2^2 = 0.81+0.81+1.8225=3.4425. Then x_5=1.35, x_5^2=1.8225, Q_5=3.4425+1.8225=5.265. That's ≤6, so no win at n=5. Then Bazza at n=6: he can choose b_3 ≤ sqrt(6 - 5.265)=sqrt0.735=0.857. He might choose b_3=0.857 to try to keep the game safe. Then S_6 = S_4 + b_3 = 3.15+0.857=3.997, Q_6 = Q_5 + 0.857^2 = 5.265+0.734=6.0. Then Alice at n=7: she can set x_7 = 6.3 - S_6 = 6.3 - 3.997 = 2.303, then Q_7 = 6 + 2.303^2 = 6+5.306=11.306 >8, win. So this seems to be a winning strategy for Alice: she can force a win at n=7 by playing x_1=0.9, then x_3=0, and then whatever Bazza does, she can win at n=7? But we need to check if Bazza can choose a different b_2 to prevent that. In the above, we used b_2=1.35. Could Bazza choose a different b_2 that makes the subsequent overflow at n=7 impossible? For instance, he might choose b_2 such that the allowed b_3 range is larger, and then he can choose b_3 to make Q_7 ≤ 8. But we saw that the minimum of Q_7 over b_3 is Q_5 + (4.5 - b_2)^2/2. For b_2=1.35, we got that value >8. Could a different b_2 yield a smaller Q_5 + (4.5-b_2)^2/2? We computed H(b_2) = 2.5b_2^2 - 9.9b_2 + 19.035, which is decreasing on [0,1.543], and at b_2=1.543, H=9.7165 >8. So the unrestricted minimum of Q_7 is >8 for all b_2 in the feasible range. But note that the actual minimum Q_7 might be achieved at the endpoint b_3=B if the vertex is not feasible. Since the vertex is > B, the minimum over the interval is at b_3=B, which we computed as M(b_2)=6+(4.5-b_2)^2-2(4.5-b_2)B. We can compute this for different b_2 to see if it can be ≤8. For b_2=1.543, M=9.938>8. For b_2=1.0, Q_5 = 2*1 -5.4*1+8.91=2-5.4+8.91=5.51, B= sqrt(0.49)=0.7, then M=6+(4.5-1)^2 -2*(3.5)*0.7=6+12.25 -4.9=13.35>8. For b_2=1.2, Q_5 = 2*1.44 -5.4*1.2+8.91=2.88-6.48+8.91=5.31, B= sqrt(0.69)=0.831, then M=6+(4.5-1.2)^2 -2*(3.3)*0.831=6+10.89 -5.486=11.404>8. So M(b_2) is >8 for all b_2. So Bazza cannot avoid that Alice can win at n=7. So for λ=0.9, Alice wins.

What about λ=0.8? Let's try a similar strategy. Alice chooses x_1=0.8 (max). Then Q_1=0.64. Bazza at n=2 can choose b_1 up to sqrt(2-0.64)=sqrt1.36=1.1662. He might choose b_1=0.8. Then S_2=1.6, Q_2=0.64+0.64=1.28. She sets x_3=0. Then S_3=1.6, Q_3=1.28. Bazza at n=4: he can choose b_2 up to sqrt(4-1.28)=sqrt2.72=1.649. He might choose b_2=1.6? Then S_4=3.2, Q_4=1.28+2.56=3.84. She sets x_7 = 6.4 - 4.433 = 1.967, then Q_7=6+3.869=9.869>8, win. So Alice wins. But can Bazza choose a different b_2 to avoid that? We need to compute the condition. For λ=0.8, we have Q_3 = 1.28 (if b_1=0.8). Then after his move at n=4, Q_4 = 1.28+b_2^2, S_4=1.6+b_2. Then Alice at n=5: a_2 = 4 - S_4 = 2.4 - b_2, Q_5 = Q_4 + a_2^2 = 1.28+b_2^2 + (2.4-b_2)^2 = 1.28 + b_2^2 + 5.76 -4.8b_2 + b_2^2 = 7.04 + 2b_2^2 -4.8b_2. Then Q_7 = Q_6 + a_3^2 = (Q_5+b_3^2) + (4.8 - b_2 - b_3)^2. She wants Q_7 > 8. We need to see if Bazza can choose b_2 to make the minimal possible Q_7 over b_3 ≤ sqrt(6 - Q_5) be ≤ 8. Compute the unrestricted minimum of g(b_3) = b_3^2 + (4.8 - b_2 - b_3)^2 = 2b_3^2 - 2(4.8 - b_2)b_3 + (4.8 - b_2)^2. Vertex at b_3 = (4.8 - b_2)/2. The minimum value is (4.8 - b_2)^2/2. So Q_7_min = Q_5 + (4.8 - b_2)^2/2. So we need to see if there exists b_2 such that Q_5 + (4.8 - b_2)^2/2 ≤ 8. Compute Q_5 = 2b_2^2 -4.8b_2 + 7.04. Then H(b_2) = 2b_2^2 -4.8b_2 + 7.04 + (1/2)(4.8 - b_2)^2 = 2b_2^2 -4.8b_2 + 7.04 + (1/2)(23.04 - 9.6b_2 + b_2^2) = 2b_2^2 -4.8b_2 + 7.04 + 11.52 - 4.8b_2 + 0.5b_2^2 = (2.5)b_2^2 -9.6b_2 + 18.56. So the minimum is at b_2=1.649, giving H(1.649)=2.5*2.719 -9.6*1.649 + 18.56 = 6.7975 -15.806 + 18.56 = 9.5515. That's >8. So the unrestricted minimum is >8. However, the actual minimum over the feasible b_3 range might be at the endpoint. Since the vertex is at b_3 = (4.8 - b_2)/2. For b_2=1.649, this is (4.8-1.649)/2=3.151/2=1.5755. But the allowed b_3 is ≤ sqrt(6 - Q_5). We need to compute Q_5 at b_2=1.649: Q_5 = 2*2.719 -4.8*1.649 + 7.04 = 5.438 -7.895 +7.04 = 4.583. Then B = sqrt(6-4.583)= sqrt1.417=1.190. Since vertex b_3=1.5755 > B, the function g(b_3) is decreasing on [0, B], so the minimum over the interval is at b_3=B. Then M(b_2)= Q_5 + g(B) = Q_5 + (2B^2 -2(4.8-b_2)B + (4.8-b_2)^2) = Q_5 + 2B^2 -2(4.8-b_2)B + (4.8-b_2)^2. But Q_5 + 2B^2 = Q_5 + 2(6 - Q_5) = 12 - Q_5. So M(b_2)=12 - Q_5 -2(4.8-b_2)B + (4.8-b_2)^2. Actually 12-7.04=4.96, +23.04=28. So M = 28 - b_2^2 -4.8b_2 -2(4.8-b_2)B. For b_2=1.649, compute 28 - (2.719) -4.8*1.649 = 28 -2.719 -7.895 = 17.386, then 2(4.8-1.649)*1.190 = 2*3.151*1.190 = 2*3.747 = 7.494, so M=17.386-7.494=9.892 >8. So again, Alice wins.

Thus for λ=0.8, Alice also wins. What about λ=0.5? Let's test. Alice at n=1: she can choose x_1=0.5. Then Q_1=0.25. Bazza at n=2: b_1 ≤ sqrt(2-0.25)=sqrt1.75=1.3229. He might choose b_1=0.5. She sets x_5 = 4 - 2.8 = 1.2, Q_5 = 3.74+1.44=5.18. Then Bazza at n=6: b_3 ≤ sqrt(6-5.18)=sqrt0.82=0.9055, he might choose b_3=0.9, then S_6=2.8+0.9=3.7, Q_6=5.18+0.81=5.99. But we need to see if there is a λ where Bazza can force a win. From our tests, it seems that for λ=0.1, Bazza wins. For λ=0.5, Alice wins. So there must be a threshold λ0 between 0.1 and 0.5 where the outcome changes. Let's try λ=0.3. Alice at n=1: she can choose x_1=0.3 (max). Q_1=0.09. Bazza at n=2: b_1 ≤ sqrt(2-0.09)=sqrt1.91=1.3819. He might choose b_1=0.3. Then S_2=0.6, Q_2=0.09+0.09=0.18. She sets x_3=0. Then S_3=0.6, Q_3=0.18. Bazza at n=4: b_2 ≤ sqrt(4-0.18)=sqrt3.82=1.954. He might choose b_2=1.9? So she wins. But could Bazza choose a different b_2 to keep Q_5 ≤6? For λ=0.3, we have Q_3=0.18 (if b_1=0.3). Then after his move at n=4, we have Q_4=0.18+b_2^2, S_4=0.6+b_2. Alice at n=5: a_2 = 4 - S_4 = 3.4 - b_2, Q_5 = Q_4 + a_2^2 = 0.18+b_2^2 + (3.4-b_2)^2 = 0.18 + b_2^2 + 11.56 -6.8b_2 + b_2^2 = 11.74 + 2b_2^2 -6.8b_2. He wants Q_5 ≤6. So b_2 must be between 1.5586 and 1.8414 to satisfy Q_5 ≤6. But also b_2 ≤ sqrt(4-0.18)=1.954. So feasible b_2 is [1.5586, 1.8414]. If he chooses b_2=1.7, then Q_5 = 11.74 + 2*2.89 -6.8*1.7 = 11.74+5.78-11.56=6.06 >6. So still >6? Let's compute precisely: b_2=1.7 => 2b_2^2=5.78, -6.8b_2=-11.56, sum=5.78-11.56=-5.78, plus 11.74=6.06. That's >6. So Q_5 >6. But the condition for Q_5 ≤6 is 2b_2^2 -6.8b_2 +11.74 ≤6, i.e., 2b_2^2 -6.8b_2 +5.74 ≤0. The quadratic 2b_2^2 -6.8b_2 +5.74 has vertex at b_2=1.7, value 2*2.89 -11.56+5.74=5.78-11.56+5.74=0. So at b_2=1.7, the quadratic is exactly 0. So Q_5 = 6? Actually compute: Q_5 = 11.74 + (2b_2^2 -6.8b_2) = 11.74 + (2*2.89 -11.56)=11.74+5.78-11.56=11.74-5.78? So if Bazza chooses b_2=1.7, then Q_5=5.96 ≤6, so he can avoid overflow at n=5. Then the game continues. Then at n=6, he will choose b_3, etc. So for λ=0.3, Bazza might be able to avoid losing by appropriate choices. Let's simulate with Bazza playing optimally. We'll need to see if Alice can still force a win later. Let's continue from that state.

We have λ=0.3. Alice sets x_1=0.3 (max). Bazza at n=2 chooses b_1=0.3 (as before). Then S_2=0.6, Q_2=0.18. Alice at n=3 chooses x_3=0 (as before). Then S_3=0.6, Q_3=0.18. We computed that if he chooses b_2=1.7, then Q_5=5.96, and then at n=6, he must choose b_3 ≤ sqrt(6-5.96)=0.2. Then the overflow at n=7 is guaranteed because the maximum x_7 she can set is 6.3 - S_6, and Q_7 will be Q_6 + (6.3 - S_6)^2. For any S_6, Q_7 = Q_6 + (6.3 - S_6)^2. The maximum Q_5 he can achieve while keeping Q_5 ≤6 is at the boundary of the inequality? Actually he wants to minimize the chance of Alice's win later. So he will choose b_2 to make the subsequent state as "safe" as possible. The safe state after his move is (S_4, Q_4). Then Alice's move at n=5 will set S_5 = 4 (since she will set a_2 to max), and Q_5 = Q_4 + (4 - S_4)^2. He wants to keep Q_5 as low as possible to give himself room later? Actually he wants to avoid that Alice can later cause overflow. But we see that if Q_5 is too low, then at n=6 he can only choose a small b_3, and then at n=7 she can overflow. So he might want to make Q_5 larger, but not too large that she wins at n=5. The condition for her not to win at n=5 is Q_5 ≤6. So he can make Q_5 up to 6. If he makes Q_5 = 6, then at n=6, he must choose b_3 = 0 (since Q_5 + b_3^2 ≤6 => b_3=0). Then S_6 = S_4, Q_6=6. Then Alice at n=7: a_3 = 6.3 - S_6, Q_7 = 6 + (6.3 - S_6)^2. So Alice wins. So it seems that for λ=0.3, Alice also wins. So maybe for all λ > 0, Alice wins? But we saw λ=0.1 gave Bazza win. Let's test λ=0.1 more systematically. Alice at n=1: she can choose x_1=0.1 (max). Then Q_1=0.01. Bazza at n=2: b_1 ≤ sqrt(2-0.01)=sqrt1.99=1.410. He might choose b_1=1.4 (max) to cause her loss? But he must respect Q_2 ≤2. So Bazza wins. So for λ=0.1, Bazza wins. So there is a threshold λ0 where the outcome changes. We need to find λ0 such that for λ > λ0, Alice has a winning strategy; for λ < λ0, Bazza has a winning strategy. At λ = λ0, the game may be a draw (or maybe one player can force a win? Need to check).

Then Bazza's turn at n=2: he chooses b_1 = x_2. Since he wants to win, he will choose b_1 to maximize his chance. He can choose any b_1 such that Q_2 = λ^2 + b_1^2 ≤ 2. He will likely choose b_1 = sqrt(2-λ^2) (the maximum allowed) to try to make S_2 large. Then S_2 = λ + sqrt(2-λ^2). Now, we need to check that S_2 < 3λ (since λ > λ0). Indeed, sqrt(2-λ^2) < 2λ because λ^2 > 2/5 implies 2-λ^2 < 2 - 2/5 = 8/5 = 4λ^2? Actually from λ^2 > 2/5, we have 2-λ^2 < 2 - 2/5 = 8/5, and (2λ)^2 = 4λ^2 > 8/5? Since 4λ^2 > 8/5? That's true if λ^2 > 2/5, which holds. So sqrt(2-λ^2) < 2λ, so S_2 = λ + sqrt(2-λ^2) < λ + 2λ = 3λ. So she can move at n=3. Then she sets x_3 = 3λ - S_2 = 3λ - (λ + sqrt(2-λ^2)) = 2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2). This is positive because sqrt(2-λ^2) < 2λ. So x_3 > 0. Then S_3 = 3λ. Then Q_3 = Q_2 + x_3^2 = (λ^2 + (sqrt(2-λ^2))^2) + (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2 = λ^2 + (2-λ^2) + (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2 = 2 + (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2. So Q_3 = 2 + (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2.

Now, Bazza's turn at n=4: he chooses b_2. He can choose any b_2 such that Q_4 = Q_3 + b_2^2 ≤ 4. So b_2^2 ≤ 4 - Q_3. Since Q_3 = 2 + (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2, we have 4 - Q_3 = 2 - (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2. Let's denote d = 2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2). Then Q_3 = 2 + d^2. So b_2^2 ≤ 2 - d^2. He wants to make S_4 = S_3 + b_2 = 3λ + b_2 > 5λ to cause Alice's loss at n=5. So he needs b_2 > 2λ. But can he choose b_2 > 2λ? Since b_2 ≤ sqrt(2 - d^2). So we need to check if sqrt(2 - d^2) > 2λ. That is, 2 - d^2 > 4λ^2 => d^2 < 2 - 4λ^2. Now d = 2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2). Compute d^2 = (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2 = 4λ^2 + (2-λ^2) - 4λ sqrt(2-λ^2) = 2 + 3λ^2 - 4λ sqrt(2-λ^2)? Actually 4λ^2 + 2 - λ^2 = 2 + 3λ^2. So d^2 = 2 + 3λ^2 - 4λ sqrt(2-λ^2). So condition d^2 < 2 - 4λ^2 becomes 2 + 3λ^2 - 4λ sqrt(2-λ^2) < 2 - 4λ^2 => 3λ^2 + 4λ^2 < 4λ sqrt(2-λ^2) => 7λ^2 < 4λ sqrt(2-λ^2) => 7λ < 4 sqrt(2-λ^2) => square: 49λ^2 < 16(2-λ^2) => 49λ^2 < 32 - 16λ^2 => 65λ^2 < 32 => λ^2 < 32/65 ≈0.4923077 => λ < 0.7018. But λ > √(2/5) ≈0.6325. So there is a range λ in (0.6325, 0.7018) where sqrt(2 - d^2) > 2λ, meaning Bazza can choose b_2 > 2λ and win at n=5. However, we also need that b_2 is allowed: b_2 ≤ sqrt(2 - d^2). If he chooses b_2 > 2λ, then S_4 = 3λ + b_2 > 5λ, so Alice loses at n=5. So for λ between √(2/5) and √(32/65), Bazza can win by playing b_2 > 2λ at n=4. But we previously tested λ=0.65 and found that Alice could win. Let's check that: λ=0.65, compute d = 2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2) = 1.3 - sqrt(2-0.4225)=1.3 - sqrt1.5775=1.3 - 1.256=0.044. Then d^2=0.00194. Then 2 - d^2 = 1.99806, sqrt=1.414, and 2λ=1.3. So sqrt > 2λ, so Bazza can choose b_2 > 2λ. He can choose b_2=1.414, then S_4=3λ+1.414=1.95+1.414=3.364, λ*5=3.25, so S_4 > 3.25, Alice loses. So for λ=0.65, Bazza can win by playing b_2=1.414. But earlier we simulated with Bazza playing b_1 = sqrt(2-λ^2)=1.256, and then Alice set x_3=0, and then Bazza set b_2=1.414, and we found that Alice loses at n=5. So that suggests that for λ=0.65, Bazza wins, not Alice. But earlier we thought Alice wins for λ=0.65. Let's re-evaluate that simulation. We had λ=0.65, Alice set x_1=0.65, then Bazza set b_1 = sqrt(2-0.4225)=1.256, then S_2=1.906, Q_2=2.0. Then Alice set x_3=0, then S_3=1.906, Q_3=2.0. Then Bazza set b_2=1.414, then S_4=1.906+1.414=3.32, λ*5=3.25, so S_4=3.32 > 3.25, so Alice cannot move at n=5. She loses. So Bazza wins. So our earlier conclusion that Alice wins for λ=0.65 was based on the assumption that Bazza would not play b_2=1.414, but we just saw he can. So we need to check if Bazza can always force a win for λ between √(2/5) and √(32/65) by playing the maximum b_2 at n=4. That would cause Alice's loss at n=5. But wait, can Alice avoid that by choosing a different x_3? At n=3, she could choose x_3 positive to make S_3 = 3λ. But then Q_3 would be larger. Let's analyze the game for λ in that range. She might try to set x_3 = 3λ - S_2 = 2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2). That is a positive number. For λ=0.65, this is 1.3 - 1.256 = 0.044. So she can set x_3=0.044. Then S_3=3λ=1.95, Q_3 = Q_2 + (0.044)^2 = 2.0 + 0.001936 = 2.001936. Then Bazza at n=4: he can choose b_2 up to sqrt(4 - Q_3) = sqrt(4 - 2.001936)= sqrt1.998064=1.4137. Then he can choose b_2 = 1.4137, then S_4=1.95+1.4137=3.3637, which is > 3.25, so she loses at n=5. So still loss. Could she choose x_3 smaller than that? She must have S_3 ≤ 3λ. If she chooses x_3=0, then S_3=1.906 < 3λ, so she can move at n=5. But then Bazza can set b_2=1.414, making S_4=3.32 >3.25, she loses at n=5. So she loses. If she chooses x_3=0.01, then S_3=1.916, Q_3=2.0+0.0001=2.0001, then b_2 max = sqrt(2 - 0.0001)=1.414, S_4=1.916+1.414=3.33 >3.25, still loss. So any choice leads to Bazza being able to cause loss at n=5. So for λ=0.65, Bazza wins. So our threshold λ0 = √(2/5) is not the only threshold; there is another critical value where the maximum b_2 at n=4 can exceed 2λ. We need to find the set of λ where Alice can force a win.

We need to analyze the game more generally. The game is a kind of "alternating control" where each player can choose to increase the sum or sum of squares. The outcome is determined by the ratio of λ. We need to find all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and all for which Bazza has a winning strategy. Possibly there are multiple critical λ values, and the answer might be: Alice wins if and only if λ > 2, Bazza wins if and only if λ < 2, and at λ=2 it's a draw. But our tests show that for λ=0.65, Bazza wins, but λ=0.65 < 2, so that would be consistent with Bazza winning for λ < 2. But we also have λ=1, which is <2, but we found Alice wins for λ=1. So that's contradictory. So maybe the threshold is λ = 2? But λ=1 gave Alice win, so that's not. Let's re-examine λ=1. For λ=1, we saw that if Bazza plays b_1 = sqrt(2-1)=1, then S_2=2, Q_2=2. Then Alice at n=3: she can set x_3 = 3 - 2 = 1 (max) or 0. If she sets x_3=0, then S_3=2, Q_3=2. Then Bazza at n=4: he can set b_2 = sqrt(4-2)=√2≈1.414, then S_4=2+1.414=3.414, and λ*5=5, so S_4=3.414 <5, so she can move at n=5. Then she sets x_5 = 5 - 3.414 = 1.586, Q_5=4+2.516=6.516, then Bazza at n=6: Q_5=6.516>6, so he loses. So Alice wins. But could Bazza do something else to avoid that? If he chooses b_2 = 0, then S_4=2, Q_4=2. Then Alice at n=5: x_5=5-2=3, Q_5=2+9=11>6, win. So any b_2 leads to Q_5 >6? Let's check: If he chooses b_2=1, then S_4=3, Q_4=3, then x_5=5-3=2, Q_5=3+4=7>6. So indeed, Alice wins. So λ=1 is winning for Alice. So the threshold must be somewhere between 0.65 and 1. We already have λ=0.65 gave Bazza win, λ=1 gave Alice win. So there is a critical λ in (0.65,1). Let's find it.

We need to analyze the game systematically. The game can be analyzed by considering the "potential" function and the "critical" λ where the safe region for Bazza changes. This is reminiscent of the "game of inequalities" where the critical λ is when the inequality relating the sum and sum of squares becomes tight. Often the answer is λ = 2, but we see that λ=1 gives Alice win. Could it be that λ > 2 is winning for Alice, and λ < 2 is winning for Bazza? But λ=1 <2, and we found Alice win. So maybe it's λ > 1? Let’s denote λ. The game can be analyzed by considering the state after each of Alice's turns. She wants to make the sum of squares exceed the even index. For her to win, she needs to be able to force a situation where after her odd turn, Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, so that Bazza cannot move at his next turn. She can influence Q by her choice of x_{2k+1}. At her turn, she must choose x_{2k+1} such that S_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). Given the state after Bazza's turn, she has S_{2k} and Q_{2k}. She can choose any x_{2k+1} ∈ [0, λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}]. She will choose x_{2k+1} to maximize her chance to win. Her win condition at that turn is that Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2 (since Bazza will have to move at n=2k+2). That is, Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2 > 2k+2. Since she can choose x_{2k+1} up to some bound, she will try to make x_{2k+1} large enough to achieve this. However, she also must ensure that she can later survive if she doesn't win immediately. So she will try to make Q as large as possible, but also she might need to keep the sum within bounds to allow her to move later.

From our earlier analysis, under the greedy strategy, we have S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1), and Q_{2k+1}=2k + h_k^2, where h_1 = 2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2), and h_{k+1} = 2λ - sqrt(2 - h_k^2). This is the recurrence for the "safety" of the game. For the game to be a draw, we need that for all k, b_{k+1} = sqrt(2 - h_k^2) ≤ 2λ, which is always true because 2 - h_k^2 ≤ 2, and 2λ is positive. But we also need that the game never terminates. Termination occurs if at some even turn, Q_{2k-1} > 2k, i.e., the sum of squares exceeds the index. Under greedy, Q_{2k-1} = 2k-2 + h_{k-1}^2. We can attempt to analyze the game using the idea of "critical λ" where the best possible play leads to a draw. The draw occurs when both players can force an infinite play. This is reminiscent of the "game of inequalities" where the critical λ is the unique solution to an equation. From our tests, it appears that for λ=0.71, the game is a draw; for λ=0.72, Alice wins. We can use the following approach: Consider the game where Alice uses the strategy: she will always set x_{odd} = λ n - S_{n-1} (the maximum) to maximize Q, and Bazza will also set x_{even} = sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}) (the maximum) to try to make S_{2k} as large as possible. Then the state after Alice's turn is given by S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1), Q_{2k+1}=2k + h_k^2, where h_k = 2λ - sqrt(2 - h_{k-1}^2). Under this, the game will continue indefinitely if h_k^2 ≤ 2 for all k (since then Q_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2, so Bazza can always move). If at some point h_k^2 > 2, then Alice wins. So the condition for a draw is that h_k^2 ≤ 2 for all k. Since h_k is decreasing, it will converge to some limit L. The limit L satisfies L = 2λ - sqrt(2 - L^2). This equation can be solved. Square: (2λ - L)^2 = 2 - L^2 => 4λ^2 - 4λL + L^2 = 2 - L^2 => 4λ^2 - 4λL + 2L^2 = 2 => 2L^2 - 4λL + (4λ^2 - 2) = 0 => L^2 - 2λL + (2λ^2 - 1) = 0. The discriminant: 4λ^2 - 4(2λ^2 - 1) = 4λ^2 - 8λ^2 + 4 = 4 - 4λ^2 = 4(1 - λ^2). So L = λ ± √(1 - λ^2). Since L is nonnegative and less than or equal to? For λ<1, we have two positive roots? Actually L = λ ± √(1 - λ^2). The positive root is λ + √(1 - λ^2) > λ. But h_1 is less than that. The limit L is the smaller root? Let's analyze. The two roots are λ ± √(1 - λ^2) = 0.8 ± √(1-0.64)=0.8 ± 0.6 = 1.4 and 0.2. So L=0.2 is the smaller root. So the limit L is λ - √(1 - λ^2) (since λ - √(1 - λ^2) is less than λ). For λ=0.8, that's 0.8 - 0.6 = 0.2. For λ=0.7, λ - √(1 - λ^2) = 0.7 - √(1-0.49)=0.7 - √0.51=0.7-0.714= -0.014, negative. We need to analyze the game. It's a kind of alternating move game with constraints. Let's denote the state after n turns. But if the sum of squares before his turn is > n, then no nonnegative x_n can make sum of squares <= n because sum will be at least that. If T_{n-1} > n, then no nonnegative x_n works. So Bazza loses if the sum of squares after Alice's turn exceeds n.

Thus the game is about controlling the sums and sum of squares. Both players can always choose 0 if the constraint is satisfied. But they might want to choose positive numbers to set up future moves.

We need to find for which λ Alice has a winning strategy (i.e., she can force Bazza to eventually be unable to move) and for which Bazza has a winning strategy (he can force Alice to be unable to move). Let's think about the constraints. The sum constraint: Alice's moves must keep the sum <= λ n at odd n. The sum of squares constraint: Bazza's moves must keep the sum of squares <= n at even n. Both are cumulative.

Consider the long-term behavior. For the game to go on forever, both players must always have a move. That means that at each odd n, A_{n-1} <= λ n, and at each even n, T_{n-1} <= n. In particular, these conditions must hold for all n.

Thus the game is akin to a tug-of-war between sum and sum of squares. It might be reminiscent of the "inequality game" or "lambda game". Possibly we can use the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality: (sum x_i)^2 <= n * (sum x_i^2). That is, A_n^2 <= n * T_n. The constraints are A_{2k+1} <= λ (2k+1) and T_{2k} <= 2k. Using the inequality, we can get bounds on the possible sums and squares.

We can attempt to analyze the game by considering the "potential" function: S_n = sum x_i, and Q_n = sum x_i^2. The constraints: At odd n, S_n <= λ n; at even n, Q_n <= n. Both players can choose 0 to not increase sums. But to force a win, they need to make the opponent's constraint impossible.

We can consider the game as a kind of "alternating control" where the players can choose to "spend" their resources. Since the sum constraint is linear and the square constraint is quadratic, we need to find the threshold where one player can always maintain their constraints and eventually force the other to break.

Better to define the state after each even turn. Let’s denote at the start of Bazza's turn at n=2k, we have A_{2k-1}=S_{2k-1} (Alice's sum after her turn) and Q_{2k-1} (sum of squares after her turn). Thus the game can be analyzed by considering the "potential" function P_k = (S_{2k-1}, Q_{2k-1}) or maybe the ratio.

The pattern suggests that if both play optimally, the game can continue indefinitely for λ=0.8. But we need to determine the critical λ where the game is balanced. Perhaps the answer is: Alice wins if λ > 2? But we already saw λ=1 gives her win. So that's not right.

Maybe we misinterpreted the game. The rule: "If n is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1 + ... + x_n ≤ λ n." That means that on her turn, she must choose x_n such that the cumulative sum up to n is ≤ λ n. But note that after Bazza's turn, the sum is not constrained. So she can choose x_n to be 0, and then the sum after her turn might be less than λ n, but then she might be able to increase later. However, the condition is only that the sum after her choice is ≤ λ n. So she can always choose 0 if the previous sum is already ≤ λ n? But the previous sum is after Bazza's turn, which could be > λ n? Actually if after Bazza's turn, the sum is > λ n, then she cannot choose any nonnegative x_n because the sum would be at least that > λ n. So she loses. So she must ensure that after Bazza's turn, the sum is ≤ λ n. That's her constraint. So the game is about controlling the sum after each of Bazza's turns to keep it within λ times the odd index. Similarly, Bazza must ensure that after his turn, the sum of squares is ≤ n, and also he must ensure that after his turn, the sum (which is used by Alice) is not too high to cause her loss.

Thus the players have control over the increments. Let's denote the state before each player's turn.

Define k = 1,2,3,... Let's consider the state after each even turn (Bazza's turn). Let n=2k. Then we have S_{2k} = sum_{i=1}^{2k} x_i, and Q_{2k} = sum_{i=1}^{2k} x_i^2. The constraint for Bazza at his turn (n=2k) is Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. But note that Q_{2k} is after his choice, so he must satisfy Q_{2k-1} + x_{2k}^2 ≤ 2k. He can choose x_{2k} to be any nonnegative number such that the inequality holds. So his move determines S_{2k} and Q_{2k}. Then Alice's turn at n=2k+1: she must choose x_{2k+1} such that S_{2k+1} ≤ λ (2k+1). She can choose any nonnegative x_{2k+1} such that S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} + x_{2k+1} ≤ λ (2k+1). So she needs S_{2k} ≤ λ (2k+1) to have any move. If S_{2k} > λ (2k+1), she loses. So Bazza wants to make S_{2k} as large as possible, but limited by the quadratic constraint. He can choose x_{2k} to increase S_{2k}. Similarly, Alice wants to make Q_{2k+1} as large as possible, so that on Bazza's next turn (n=2k+2), she can cause Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, making him lose. But she also must keep S_{2k+1} ≤ λ (2k+1). So she can try to increase Q_{2k+1} by choosing a large x_{2k+1}. However, she is limited by the sum constraint: she can choose x_{2k+1} up to λ (2k+1) - S_{2k}. So she can increase Q by that amount, but the quadratic contribution is x^2. So she can choose x_{2k+1} = λ (2k+1) - S_{2k} (the maximum allowed) to make S_{2k+1} = λ (2k+1) exactly. This is often optimal for her because it gives her the largest possible Q increase. So she will likely set S_{2k+1} = λ (2k+1) (if possible). Then Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + (λ (2k+1) - S_{2k})^2. So the state after her turn is determined by the previous state.

Thus the game can be analyzed by the recurrence:

Let s_k = S_{2k} (after Bazza's turn), q_k = Q_{2k} (after Bazza's turn). Bazza chooses x_{2k} (call it b_k) such that q_k + b_k^2 ≤ 2k. Then he sets s_k = S_{2k-1} + b_k, and q_k = Q_{2k-1} + b_k^2? Wait careful: The state after Bazza's turn is (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}). But the state before his turn is (S_{2k-1}, Q_{2k-1}). He chooses b_k = x_{2k} such that Q_{2k-1} + b_k^2 ≤ 2k. Then S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + b_k, Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + b_k^2 (he can choose to make Q_{2k} exactly 2k if he wants, but he might also choose less). So after his turn, we have s_k = S_{2k}, q_k = Q_{2k}. Then Alice's turn: she chooses a_k = x_{2k+1} such that S_{2k+1} = s_k + a_k ≤ λ (2k+1). She will likely set a_k = λ (2k+1) - s_k (maximum allowed) to maximize Q increase. Then Q_{2k+1} = q_k + a_k^2. Then Bazza's next turn: he sees state (S_{2k+1}, Q_{2k+1}) and chooses b_{k+1}. So we have a recurrence.

Thus we can consider the game as a two-player game where each player chooses a number that determines the next state. The players want to force a win: Alice wants to eventually make Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2 (so that Bazza cannot move at n=2k+2). Bazza wants to eventually make S_{2k} > λ (2k+1) (so that Alice cannot move at n=2k+1). But note that if Bazza makes S_{2k} > λ (2k+1), then Alice loses immediately at her next turn. So Bazza's winning condition is that at some k, S_{2k} > λ (2k+1). Similarly, Alice's winning condition is that at some k, Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2.

Now we need to find for which λ Alice has a strategy to force Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2 for some k, regardless of Bazza's play. And for which λ Bazza has a strategy to force S_{2k} > λ (2k+1) for some k, regardless of Alice's play.

If both players play optimally, the game may be a draw if neither can force a win indefinitely.

We can try to find the threshold λ0 such that for λ > λ0, Alice can force a win, and for λ < λ0, Bazza can force a win. At λ = λ0, the game is a draw.

From our experiments: λ=1 gave Alice win, λ=0.8 gave a draw (maybe), λ=0.1 gave Bazza win. So λ0 is somewhere between 0.8 and 1? But we also saw λ=1.2 gave Alice win. Then Alice at n=7: x_7 = 6.3 - 5.395 = 0.905, Q_7=6+0.819=6.819, S_7=6.3. Then Bazza at n=8: up to sqrt(8-6.819)=sqrt1.181=1.087, S_8=6.3+1.087=7.387, Q_8=6.819+1.181=8.0. It seems that for λ=0.9, the game might continue indefinitely if both play the maximum allowed. But we haven't proven that Bazza can avoid losing. However, we need to see if Alice can force a win by some other strategy. Perhaps she can set x_1 to something less than 0.9 to allow a different pattern. But she wants to maximize her chance to overflow. The recurrence might be analyzed.

Let's try to find the critical λ where Alice can always eventually make Q_{odd} > 2k+2. This is reminiscent of a known inequality game: For a given λ, the maximal possible ratio of sum of squares to sum of linear sum? Actually we have constraints: On Alice's turns, S_n ≤ λ n; on Bazza's turns, Q_n ≤ n. We can think of the "score" as the difference between the potential and the constraints.

Another approach: Consider the potential function F_n = (sum x_i)^2 - λ n sum x_i^2? Not sure.

We can try to derive a necessary condition for Bazza to avoid losing indefinitely. If Bazza can always make a move, then we must have for all k, S_{2k} ≤ λ (2k+1) (so Alice can move). Also, Q_{2k} ≤ 2k (by his move). And he can choose his moves to maintain these. Alice can choose her moves to try to increase Q_{2k+1} beyond 2k+2.

Suppose Bazza plays optimally to avoid losing. He might choose x_{2k} to minimize the chance that Alice can overflow at the next odd turn. That is, he wants to make S_{2k} as large as possible? Actually if he makes S_{2k} large, that could cause Alice to lose at her next turn if S_{2k} > λ (2k+1). But he also wants to avoid that Alice can later make Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2. So he might want to keep Q_{2k} small, but he can also choose x_{2k} to influence both. Let's analyze the recurrence.

We can define the "score" for Alice after her turn: Q_{2k+1} - (2k+2). She wants this to be positive. Given Q_{2k} and S_{2k}, she can choose a = x_{2k+1} ∈ [0, λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}]. The maximum Q she can achieve is Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2. So she will try to make Q_{2k+1} as large as possible. So we can define the "overflow" value O_k = Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2 - (2k+2). She wants O_k > 0 for some k. Bazza wants to avoid that for all k.

Bazza's move: given Q_{2k-1} and S_{2k-1}, he chooses b = x_{2k} ∈ [0, sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1})]. He will choose b to minimize the maximum possible overflow that Alice can cause at the next turn. That is, he wants to minimize the maximum over his choice of the quantity Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2 - (2k+2). But note that S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + b, and Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + b^2. So we have a function f(b) = (Q_{2k-1} + b^2) + (λ(2k+1) - (S_{2k-1} + b))^2 - (2k+2). He wants to choose b to minimize the maximum? Actually he wants to avoid that Alice can make overflow > 0. So he wants to make sure that for the b he chooses, the maximum possible overflow (by Alice) is ≤ 0. Since Alice will then choose a to maximize Q, he must ensure that even if Alice chooses the maximum a, the overflow is ≤ 0. So his condition is: he must choose b such that Q_{2k-1} + b^2 + (λ(2k+1) - (S_{2k-1} + b))^2 ≤ 2k+2. If he can choose b satisfying that, then Alice cannot overflow at that turn. He also must respect that b^2 ≤ 2k - Q_{2k-1}. So he wants to see if there exists b ∈ [0, sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1})] such that the inequality holds. If there is no such b, then Alice can force a win at that turn. So the game reduces to: given the state (S_{2k-1}, Q_{2k-1}), can Bazza choose b to satisfy the inequality? If not, Alice wins. Conversely, if Bazza can choose b such that the inequality holds, then the game continues. Alice's turn then determines the next state (S_{2k+1}, Q_{2k+1}) = (λ(2k+1), Q_{2k-1}+b^2+(λ(2k+1)-S_{2k-1}-b)^2). But note that after Alice's turn, we have S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1), Q_{2k+1} = q_{2k-1} + b^2 + a^2, where a = λ(2k+1)-S_{2k-1}-b. So the state after her turn is determined.

Thus we can view the game as a deterministic process if both players play optimally: Bazza chooses b to minimize the chance of overflow, and Alice then sets a to maximize Q. So the critical λ is the one where the inequality is always satisfiable (i.e., Bazza can avoid losing) for all k, but if λ is larger, then at some point Bazza cannot avoid.

We can try to find a condition on λ such that there exists a strategy for Bazza to keep the game going forever. That would mean that for all k, there exists b ∈ [0, sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1})] such that the inequality holds. Conversely, if for some λ, no matter what Bazza does, Alice can force an overflow at some k, then Alice wins.

Perhaps it's easier to think in terms of "potential" and use the idea of "alternating inequalities" to determine the outcome. This is reminiscent of a known problem: "Alice and Bob play a game where Alice chooses x_n with sum ≤ λn, Bob chooses x_n with sum of squares ≤ n. Determine λ." Let's re-read the problem statement: "On the nth turn of the game (starting with n=1) the following happens:
- If n is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1 + x_2 + ... + x_n ≤ λ n.
- If n is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1^2 + x_2^2 + ... + x_n^2 ≤ n."
If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

So the game is an infinite game; we want to find λ where Alice can force a win (i.e., she can ensure that Bazza eventually cannot move) or Bazza can force a win.

Let's analyze from the state after Alice's first turn at n=3 (S_3=1.7, Q_3=1.45). Bazza chooses b_2 ∈ [0, sqrt(4-1.45)=sqrt2.55=1.597]. He will choose b_2 to minimize the chance that Alice can later cause overflow. The state after his move: S_4 = 1.7+b_2, Q_4 = 1.45+b_2^2. Then Alice at n=5: she can choose x_5 = 4.5 - S_4 (maximum) to maximize Q_5. Then Q_5 = Q_4 + (4.5 - (1.7+b_2))^2 = Q_4 + (2.8 - b_2)^2 = 1.45 + b_2^2 + (2.8 - b_2)^2. So Q_5 = 1.45 + b_2^2 + 7.84 - 5.6 b_2 + b_2^2 = 9.29 + 2b_2^2 - 5.6 b_2. Then Bazza at n=6: he can choose b_3 ∈ [0, sqrt(6 - Q_5)]. He wants to avoid that Alice can overflow at n=7, i.e., Q_7 = Q_6 + (6.3 - S_6)^2 > 8? Actually condition for Alice win at n=7 is Q_7 > 8. After his move at n=6, we have S_6 = S_4 + b_3 = 1.7+b_2+b_3, Q_6 = Q_5 + b_3^2 = 9.29 + 2b_2^2 - 5.6 b_2 + b_3^2. Then Alice at n=7: she can choose x_7 = 6.3 - S_6 (maximum) and then Q_7 = Q_6 + (6.3 - (1.7+b_2+b_3))^2 = Q_6 + (4.6 - b_2 - b_3)^2. For her to win, she needs Q_7 > 8. But note that she could also choose a smaller x_7 if it helps her win? Actually she wants to maximize Q_7 to cause overflow. So she will set x_7 = 6.3 - S_6 (if that is nonnegative). So her win condition is:

Q_6 + (4.6 - b_2 - b_3)^2 > 8.

Given that Bazza can choose b_3, he wants to make sure that for all possible b_3 within his allowed range, this inequality does NOT hold (or at least he can choose b_3 to avoid it). But he can choose b_3 to minimize the maximum possible Q_7? Actually he will choose b_3 to try to keep Q_7 ≤ 8. Since Alice will then choose x_7 to maximize Q_7, he must ensure that even with her best choice, Q_7 ≤ 8. So his condition is: there exists b_3 ∈ [0, sqrt(6 - Q_5)] such that Q_6 + (4.6 - b_2 - b_3)^2 ≤ 8. Since Q_6 = Q_5 + b_3^2, this is equivalent to Q_5 + b_3^2 + (4.6 - b_2 - b_3)^2 ≤ 8. He wants to choose b_2 (his move at n=4) to ensure that such a b_3 exists. So we need to see if Bazza can choose b_2 to make the minimal possible value of Q_5 + b_3^2 + (4.6 - b_2 - b_3)^2 over b_3 ∈ [0, sqrt(6 - Q_5)] be ≤ 8. Since he can choose b_2, he might be able to set it so that the minimal value is ≤ 8. But if for all b_2, the minimal value is > 8, then Alice can win. So we need to find the condition on λ (here 0.9) such that Bazza has a strategy to keep the game safe indefinitely.

This is getting complicated. Perhaps there is a known result: The critical λ is 2. Let's test λ=2 with Alice's first move: she can choose x_1=2? But then Q_1=4, Bazza at n=2 cannot move because Q_1>2, so Alice wins. So λ=2 is winning for Alice. λ=1 we found Alice win. λ=0.8 we need to check if Alice can win. Maybe there is a threshold λ0 such that for λ > λ0, Alice wins; for λ < λ0, Bazza wins; at λ=λ0, it's a draw. And from our tests, λ=1 gave Alice win, λ=0.9 might still give Alice win? Let's try to see if for λ=0.9, Alice can force a win by some strategy. He could choose b_2 not 0.9, but something else to make the allowed b_3 larger so that Alice cannot overflow at n=7. Let's analyze that.

We have λ=0.9, Alice plays x_1=0.9, then S_1=0.9, Q_1=0.81. Bazza at n=2: he can choose any b_1 ∈ [0, sqrt(2-0.81)=sqrt1.19=1.091]. He wants to avoid losing. Suppose he chooses b_1=0.9 (as before). He wants to avoid that Alice can cause overflow later. He can choose b_2 to minimize the eventual overflow. After his move, S_4 = 1.8+b_2, Q_4 = 1.62+b_2^2. Then Alice at n=5: she will choose a_2 = 4.5 - S_4 = 4.5 - (1.8+b_2) = 2.7 - b_2, and then Q_5 = Q_4 + (2.7-b_2)^2 = 1.62+b_2^2 + (2.7-b_2)^2 = 1.62 + b_2^2 + 7.29 - 5.4b_2 + b_2^2 = 8.91 + 2b_2^2 - 5.4b_2. So Q_5 = 2b_2^2 - 5.4b_2 + 8.91. The minimum of this quadratic is at b_2=1.35, giving value 2*1.8225 - 7.29 + 8.91 = 3.645 - 7.29 + 8.91 = 5.265. But note that b_2 is limited to ≤ sqrt(4-1.62)=1.543. So the minimum occurs within the interval. So if Bazza chooses b_2=1.35, then Q_5=5.265. Then at n=6, Bazza can choose b_3 ∈ [0, sqrt(6-5.265)=sqrt0.735=0.857]. Then he will choose b_3 to keep the eventual overflow at n=7. The state after his move: S_6 = S_4 + b_3 = 1.8+b_2+b_3 = 1.8+1.35+b_3 = 3.15+b_3, Q_6 = Q_5 + b_3^2 = 5.265+b_3^2. Then Alice at n=7: she can choose x_7 = 6.3 - S_6 = 6.3 - (3.15+b_3) = 3.15 - b_3, and then Q_7 = Q_6 + (3.15 - b_3)^2 = 5.265+b_3^2 + (3.15-b_3)^2 = 5.265+b_3^2 + 9.9225 - 6.3b_3 + b_3^2 = 15.1875 + 2b_3^2 - 6.3b_3. The minimum of this quadratic is at b_3=1.575, but b_3 is limited to ≤0.857. So the function is decreasing on [0,0.857] (since vertex at 1.575 > 0.857), so the minimum is at b_3=0.857, giving Q_7 = 15.1875 + 2*0.734 - 6.3*0.857 = 15.1875 + 1.468 - 5.398 = 11.2775. That's >8, so Alice wins. Could Bazza choose a different b_2 to make the vertex of the quadratic at n=7 fall within the allowed range? He wants to make the minimum possible Q_7 ≤ 8, so that he can choose b_3 to achieve Q_7 ≤ 8. Since he can choose b_2, he might set b_2 such that the allowed b_3 range is larger, and the minimum of Q_7 over b_3 becomes ≤8. Let's analyze the condition.

We have Q_5 as a function of b_2: Q_5(b_2) = 2b_2^2 - 5.4b_2 + 8.91. Then at n=6, Bazza chooses b_3 ∈ [0, sqrt(6 - Q_5)]. He will choose b_3 to minimize Q_7 = Q_6 + (4.6 - b_2 - b_3)^2? Wait we need to be careful: S_4 = 1.8+b_2, and after his move at n=6, S_6 = S_4 + b_3 = 1.8+b_2+b_3. Then Alice at n=7: she will set x_7 = 6.3 - S_6 = 6.3 - (1.8+b_2+b_3) = 4.5 - b_2 - b_3. Since Q_5 depends on b_2, we can treat this as a minimization problem: For a given b_2, let M(b_2) = min_{b_3 ∈ [0, sqrt(6 - Q_5)} [Q_5 + b_3^2 + (4.5 - b_2 - b_3)^2]. We can compute the function inside. Let’s denote g(b_3) = b_3^2 + (4.5 - b_2 - b_3)^2 = b_3^2 + (4.5 - b_2)^2 - 2(4.5 - b_2)b_3 + b_3^2 = 2b_3^2 - 2(4.5 - b_2)b_3 + (4.5 - b_2)^2. The unrestricted minimum is at b_3 = (4.5 - b_2)/2. So the minimum over the interval is at the vertex if it lies in the interval; otherwise at the endpoint. So M(b_2) = Q_5 + min_{b_3 ∈ [0, B]} g(b_3), where B = sqrt(6 - Q_5). The minimum of g(b_3) over the interval is at most the vertex value. So we have:

M(b_2) ≤ Q_5 + (4.5 - b_2)^2/2.

Thus if Bazza can choose b_2 such that Q_5 + (4.5 - b_2)^2/2 ≤ 8, then he can achieve Q_7 ≤ 8 by choosing b_3 = (4.5 - b_2)/2, provided that this b_3 is allowed (i.e., (4.5 - b_2)/2 ≤ B). But we need to check the condition.

Let's compute Q_5 as a function of b_2: Q_5 = 2b_2^2 - 5.4b_2 + 8.91. Then compute:

So H(b_2) = 2.5b_2^2 - 9.9b_2 + 19.035. This is a quadratic in b_2 with vertex at b_2 = 9.9/(5) = 1.98, but b_2 is limited to ≤1.543 (since sqrt(4-1.62)=1.543). So on the allowed interval [0,1.543], H(b_2) is decreasing? Let's check derivative: 5b_2 - 9.9, which is negative for b_2 < 1.98, so H is decreasing on [0,1.543]. So the minimum on the interval is at the right endpoint b_2=1.543, giving H(1.543) = 2.5*(2.381) - 9.9*1.543 + 19.035 = 5.9525 - 15.271 + 19.035 = 9.7165. That's >8. For b_2=1.543, this is (4.5-1.543)/2=2.957/2=1.4785. But the allowed B = sqrt(6 - Q_5). We need to compute Q_5 at b_2=1.543: Q_5 = 2*(2.381) - 5.4*1.543 + 8.91 = 4.762 - 8.332 + 8.91 = 5.34. We need to compute that. g(B) = B^2 + (4.5 - b_2 - B)^2 = B^2 + (4.5 - b_2)^2 - 2(4.5-b_2)B + B^2 = 2B^2 - 2(4.5-b_2)B + (4.5-b_2)^2. But note that Q_5 + B^2 = Q_5 + (6 - Q_5) = 6. So Q_5 + B^2 = 6. Could a smaller b_2 give a smaller M? Since M(b_2) might be decreasing? Let's compute for b_2=0: Q_5 = 2*0 - 0 + 8.91 = 8.91, then B = sqrt(6-8.91) is imaginary, not allowed because Q_5 > 6. So b_2 must be such that Q_5 ≤ 6. That is, 2b_2^2 - 5.4b_2 + 8.91 ≤ 6 => 2b_2^2 - 5.4b_2 + 2.91 ≤ 0. So Bazza cannot avoid that Alice can overflow at n=7. So for λ=0.9, Alice has a winning strategy: she can play x_1=0.9, then x_3=0, and then whatever Bazza does, she can win at n=7. Compute the unrestricted minimum of g(b_3) = b_3^2 + (4.8 - b_2 - b_3)^2 = 2b_3^2 - 2(4.8 - b_2)b_3 + (4.8 - b_2)^2. Vertex at b_3 = (4.8 - b_2)/2. The minimum value is (4.8 - b_2)^2/2. We need to analyze the game more generally. The game is a kind of "alternating control" where each player can choose to increase the sum or sum of squares. The outcome is determined by the ratio of λ. We need to find all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and all for which Bazza has a winning strategy. We already have λ=0.65 gave Bazza win, λ=1 gave Alice win. So there is a critical λ in (0.65,1). Let's find it.

We need to analyze the game systematically. The game can be analyzed by considering the "potential" function and the "critical" λ where the safe region for Bazza changes. This is reminiscent of the "game of inequalities" where the critical λ is when the inequality relating the sum and sum of squares becomes tight. Often the answer is λ = 2, but we see that λ=1 gives Alice win. Could it be that λ > 2 is winning for Alice, and λ < 2 is winning for Bazza? But λ=1 <2, and we found Alice win. So maybe it's λ > 1? But λ=0.65 <1 and Bazza win, λ=1 Alice win. So threshold at 1. But we also have λ=0.8, which is <1, but we found Alice win? Let's check λ=0.8 with Bazza playing optimally. For λ=0.8, compute λ0 = √(2/5) ≈0.6325, so λ=0.8 >0.6325. But earlier we thought Bazza can win by playing b_1 = sqrt(2-λ^2) = sqrt(2-0.64)=sqrt1.36=1.1662, then S_2=0.8+1.1662=1.9662, and λ*3=2.4, so S_2 < 2.4, so she can move at n=3. Then she sets x_3 = 2.4 - 1.9662 = 0.4338, S_3=2.4, Q_3= Q_2 + x_3^2 = 2 + 0.188 = 2.188. Then Bazza at n=4: he can set b_2 = sqrt(4 - 2.188)=sqrt1.812=1.346, then S_4=2.4+1.346=3.746, and λ*5=4, so S_4<4. We'll structure the proof as follows:

First, note that the game is equivalent to: at each turn, the player must choose a nonnegative number such that the cumulative sum (or sum of squares) satisfies a bound. The game ends if a player cannot make a move.

We'll analyze the first few moves.

Let λ0 = √(1/2). We'll treat λ < λ0 and λ ≥ λ0.

For λ < λ0, we present a strategy for Bazza to win.

We need to handle the case λ < √(2/5) separately, because he can win immediately at his first turn (n=2). For λ between √(2/5) and λ0, he can win at his second turn (n=4). So we need a unified strategy: Bazza will choose his moves to make the sum S_{2k} as large as possible, and at some point, S_{2k} > λ (2k+1), causing Alice to lose.

We can show that for any λ < λ0, there exists a k such that after Alice's odd turn, the sum of squares Q_{2k+1} is less than 2(k+1) - 4λ^2 (since 2-4λ^2 > 0). And then Bazza can choose b_{k+1} = sqrt(2(k+1) - Q_{2k+1}) > 2λ, causing S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + b_{k+1} > λ(2k+3) = λ(2(k+1)+1), so Alice loses at her next turn.

But we need to construct a winning strategy for Bazza that works for all λ < λ0. Let’s denote λ. The game can be analyzed by considering the state after each of Alice's turns. She wants to make the sum of squares exceed the even index. For her to win, she needs to be able to force a situation where after her odd turn, Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, so that Bazza cannot move at his next turn. She can influence Q by her choice of x_{2k+1}. At her turn, she must choose x_{2k+1} such that S_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). Given the state after Bazza's turn, she has S_{2k} and Q_{2k}. She can choose any x_{2k+1} ∈ [0, λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}]. She will choose x_{2k+1} to maximize her chance to win. Her win condition at that turn is that Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2 (since Bazza will have to move at n=2k+2). That is, Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2 > 2k+2. Since she can choose x_{2k+1} up to some bound, she will try to make x_{2k+1} large enough to achieve this. However, she also must ensure that she can later survive if she doesn't win immediately. So she will try to make Q as large as possible, but also she might need to keep the sum within bounds to allow her to move later.

The proof often uses the idea of "mirroring" or "invariant". Let's try to develop a proof.

- For odd n: S_n ≤ λ n.
- For even n: T_n ≤ n.

The game ends when a player cannot make a move.

We want to find λ such that one player can force a win.
